Review
Use of medroxyprogesterone acetate for hormone therapy in
postmenopausal women: Is it safe?
Frank Z. Stanczyka,c,∗, Bhagu R. Bhavnanib
a Department of Obstetrics and Gynecology, University of Southern California Keck School of Medicine, Los Angeles, CA 90033, USA
b Department of Obstetrics and Gynecology, University of Toronto and The Keenan Research Center of Li Ka Shing Knowledge Institute, St. Michael’s
Hospital, Toronto, Ont., Canada M5B 1W8
c Department of Preventive Medicine, University of Southern California Keck School of Medicine, Los Angeles, CA 90033, USA
a r t i c l e i n f o
Article history:
Received 6 July 2013
Accepted 18 November 2013
Available online 26 November 2013
Keywords:
Medroxyprogesterone acetate
Breast cancer
Androgen receptor
Cardiovascular disease
Neuroprotection
a b s t r a c t
Medroxyprogesterone acetate (MPA) has been in clinical use for over 30 years, and was generally considered to be safe until the results of long-term studies of postmenopausal hormone therapy (HT) using
treatment with conjugated equine estrogens (CEE) combined with MPA and CEE alone suggested that
MPA, and perhaps other progestogens, may play a role in the increased risk of breast cancer and cardiovascular diseases. This review examines critically the safety of MPA in terms of breast cancer and
cardiovascular disease risk, and its effects on brain function. Research into mechanisms by which MPA
might cause adverse effects in these areas, combined with the available clinical evidence, suggests a
small increase in relative risk for breast cancer and stroke, and a decline in cognitive function, in older
women using MPA with an estrogen for postmenopausal HT. However, short-term (less than 5 years) use
of MPA with an estrogen in the years immediately after the onset of menopause for the management of
vasomotor symptoms does not appear to be associated with any increased risk of these disorders.

1. Introduction
Medroxyprogesterone acetate (MPA) is a synthetic progestogen
(progestin) that is related in chemical structure to progesterone.
Its structure differs from progesterone only in that it has a methyl
group at carbon 6 and an acetate group at carbon 17. These structural differences result in a higher progestational activity and
bioavailability of MPA than progesterone when given orally.
ThemostcommonuseofMPAisforcontraceptionandHT.However
, it is also used to treat abnormal uterine bleeding, secondary
amenorrhea,dysmenorrheaandendometriosis.PriortouseofMPA
for HT and contraception, MPA in massive doses (grams) had been
used extensively for the treatment of hormone-dependent cancers,
particularlybreastcancer[1].Discussionoftheseimportantaspects
of MPA use is outside the scope of the current review. However,
a detailed review [1] has identified several independent studies.
The studies indicated in this publication show that even at very
high doses administered for an extended period of time, MPA was
relatively safe to use therapeutically for those indications.
MPA began its history of prescriptive use for postmenopausal
women in the late 1970s, after it was shown that addition of a
progestin to estrogen therapy in women with an intact uterus
could prevent estrogen-induced endometrial changes, including
adenocarcinoma. Because of its anti-proliferative effects on the
endometrium and its accepted use for other clinical applications,
MPA for more than 25 years was generally considered safe for use
in postmenopausal hormone therapy (HT). Its use rose steadily and
remained high throughout the late 1980s and 1990s. However,
this changed with the well-publicized findings of the Women’s
Health Initiative (WHI) trial in which the conjugated equine estrogen (CEE)/MPA arm of the study was prematurely stopped in 2002.
The data Safety Monitoring Board of the trial concluded that the
evidence for breast cancer harm, along with evidence for some
increaseincoronaryheartdisease,strokeandpulmonaryembolism
outweighed the evidence of benefit for fractures and possible benefit for colon cancer after a mean of 5.2 years of follow-up in
postmenopausal women [2]. Since the CEE-alone arm of that trial
showed no increase in breast cancer and the health benefits/risk
ratio was more balanced [3], a number of investigators speculated
that continuous use of MPA in the CEE/MPA trial may have played
a role in the increased risk of breast cancer.
The purpose of the present article is to review critically some
of the most important publications that address the safety of MPA,
not only regarding breast cancer risk, but also risk of cardiovascular disease and its effects on the brain when used in conjunction
with estrogens. Detailed review of the vast body of epidemiological
studiesisoutsidethescopeofthisreview,butasubstantialnumber
of these studies are discussed in several articles in this special issue
of the journal.
2. Metabolism and pharmacokinetics of MPA
The relative binding affinity profile of MPA shows that MPA
binds with high affinity to the progesterone receptor (PR) [4].
In addition, it binds substantially to both the androgen receptor
(AR) and glucocorticoid receptor. Furthermore, MPA does not bind
significantly to either the mineralocorticoid receptor or estrogen
receptor (ER).
Considering the wide use of MPA for a considerable number
of years, it is surprising that relatively little is known about the
metabolism and pharmacokinetics of MPA. The 6-methyl and 17-
acetoxy groups on the MPA molecule make it more resistant to
hepatic metabolism than progesterone. Based on in vitro studies,
three main hydroxylation sites of MPA were proposed to be 6, 2
and 1 positions, generated by CYP3A [5]. One would also expect
the double bond and ketone group in ring A to undergo reduction,
forming dihydro and tetrahydro metabolites of MPA.
Following its administration, MPA is bound weakly to albumin
in blood; it does not bind to SHBG. However, MPA appears to have
a suppressive effect on SHBG, probably due to its androgenic properties. In one study, it was shown that MPA decreased SHBG levels
by 14–18% following oral administration of 10mg MPA daily for
14 days to postmenopausal women receiving 4mg oral estradiol
valerate [6].
When a single dose of 10mg of MPA was administered orally to
each of 3 postmenopausal women, mean peak serum MPA levels
ranged from 3.4 to 4.4ng/ml and were attained between 1 and 4h
after its ingestion [7]. The MPA levels then fell precipitously until
6–12h after dosing, and subsequently declined gradually until 24h
post-treatment, at which time the MPA levels ranged from 0.3 to
0.6ng/ml.
Serum levels of MPA were measured in a single-blind, triple
cross-over study, in 12 postmenopausal women who received 1
of 3 different formulations containing MPA on each of 3 study days
separated by 1 week [8]. One of the formulations contained 5mg
of MPA combined with 2mg of estradiol. The second formulation
also contained 5mg of MPA, but was administered separately in
combination with 2mg of estradiol. The third formulation contained 10mg of MPA combined with 2mg of estradiol valerate.
The initial objective was to administer the medications using a
pre-determined randomized scheme with a balanced design to
include 5 participants who ingested each drug on each study day.
However, due to unexpected exclusion of some participants, the
number of women treated with the medications was 3–6 on the
first treatment day and 3–5 on the next two treatment days. The
results showed that there was a significant increase in MPA levels
between study days 1 and 3 with each formulation. After adjusting for inter-subject variability and period effect, the maximum
concentration (Cmax) and area under the concentration curve from
0 to 32h (AUC0–32h) values (geometric means) for the two 5-mg
MPAtreatmentswere4.2–4.4ng/mland30.6–30.8ngh/ml,respectively. For the 10-mg MPA dose, the corresponding values were
6.0ng/ml and 46.7ngh/ml, respectively. No linear-response effect
of MPA was observed in either of the two pharmacokinetic parameters studied. Similar findings were observed in a study in which
5 normally cycling women received a 10mg oral dose of MPA [9].
The elimination half-life of MPA was found to be about 30h.
It is well recognized that aging is associated with altered
pharmacokinetics of drugs. Elderly postmenopausal women are
a different population compared to younger postmenopausal
women due to age-associated changes. Pharmacokinetic differences between those age groups can be attributed to decreased
hepatic and renal function, reduced cardiac output, impaired pulmonary function, reduction in body weight and muscle mass, and
changesinbodycomposition.Ithasbeenshownthatfollowingoral
administration of 2.5 or 5mg of MPA combined with 1 or 2mg of
estradiol valerate to postmenopausal women daily for 12 or 14
days, the AUC was, on average, 1.6–1.8 times higher in the oldest group of women (>65 years) compared to the youngest group
(<60 years) [10]. This increased MPA exposure in elderly postmenopausal women may have adverse effects, especially if the
women have preexisting conditions.
3. Effect of MPA on breast cancer risk
3.1. Androgen signaling in the breast
A number of studies carried out by Wayne Tilley and Steven
Birrell and their co-workers have focused on the disruption of AR
signaling by MPA in breast tissues. These researchers believe that
(8.0.0.2542,1848623320 PDF Extractor SDK TRIAL)
32 F.Z. Stanczyk, B.R. Bhavnani / Journal of Steroid Biochemistry & Molecular Biology 142 (2014) 30–38
androgen activity is a critical opponent of estrogen activity in normal breast tissue throughout a woman’s lifespan, and that this
represents an important homeostatic mechanism which counterbalances the proliferative stimulus of estrogen on breast epithelial
cells[11].Accordingtothishormonalhomeostasismodel,suppression or disruption of AR signaling would upset the regulation of
normal breast growth and thereby predispose the tissue to development and/or promotion of breast cancer. However, there are
notable deficiencies in the studies that arrive at this conclusion.
Some of the more relevant concerns will now be discussed.
Reports by Tilley’s group assume that the androgen signaling
pathway plays a critical protective role in breast cancer growth
and that the inhibitory effect is mediated by the AR, potentially
due to induction of apoptosis [11]. However, there has been no
clear consensus from epidemiologic studies regarding exogenous
use of androgens and breast cancer risk [12]. Also, although a number of breast cancer studies have shown that endogenous levels of
estrogenandtestosteroneexhibitapositivecorrelationwithbreast
cancer risk, relatively few studies have controlled for estrogen as a
possibleconfounderwhenstudyingtheeffectoftestosteroneonthe
breast, despite the fact that estrogens are derived from androgens
[12].
To support their theory that AR signaling prevents the developmentofbreastcancer,Birrelletal.[11]haveciteddatafromstudies
that showed an association between AR expression and increased
survival of women with breast cancer. However, another group
showednocorrelationbetweenARexpressionandtumorgradeand
stage [13]. Additional rationale given by Burrell et al. [11] to support their view that the AR mediates growth-inhibitory effects on
breastcancercellsisderivedfromthetherapeuticefficacyofMPAat
high doses, which are used beneficially in the treatment of breast
cancers that failed to respond to chemotoxic or other endocrine
therapies. It has been reported that the response of breast tumors
tohigh-doseMPAtherapyisdependentontheexpressionoftheAR
and not the concentration of the PR [14], and that the progressionfree interval in response to MPA is directly proportional to the AR
concentration in the primary tumor [14,15]. In addition, a correlation was shown between inactivating mutations in the AR gene in
breast tumors and the failure of second-line therapy [16].
Of particular importance regarding the relationship between
MPA and the AR just discussed is the binding of MPA to the AR.
Tilley and Birrell’s group have reported that the affinity of MPA
for the AR is comparable to that of dihydrotestosterone (DHT) in
studies using breast cancer cell lines [17]. The cell lines included
ZR-75-1 cells, which express the ER, PR, AR and GR, and MDA-MB-
453 cells, which lack the ER and PR but express high levels of AR.
Using increasing concentrations (0–100nM) of 3H-DHT or 3H-MPA
in the presence of 200-fold excess of triamcinolone acetonide (TA),
which blocks binding to the PR and GR, DHT and MPA were bound
to the cytosol fractions of ZR-75-1 cells with a Kd (mean±S.E.) of
0.46±0.11 and 0.14±0.2nM, respectively. A similar experiment
with cytosol fractions of MDA-MB-453 cells showed that the Kds
for DHT and MPA were similar (0.23±0.02 and 0.24±0.06nM,
respectively). Additional experiments were carried out with the
ZR-75-1 and MDA-MB-453 cells transfected with a MMTV-CAT
reporter gene construct, and the data showed MPA-induced stimulation of androgen-responsive reporter gene activity in these cells
that was similar to that observed for CV-1 cells co-transfected with
wild-type AR. Based on these findings, it was concluded that MPA
is an AR agonist, and the suggestion was made that the in vivo
effects of MPA in breast cancer patients may be partly mediated
by the AR. However, in a study by Kemppainen et al. [18], it was
shown that MPA is an AR agonist in transcription activation assays
but requires 100-fold higher concentrations than DHT and 10-fold
higher concentrations than testosterone. Also, MPA showed no AR
antagonistic activity when tested in CV1 cells by coincubation with
0.1nM DHT. Thus, MPA showed some androgenic activity but no
anti-androgenic activity in the presence of DHT.
Birrell et al. suggested that MPA at low doses (<10nM), such as
those used for postmenopausal HT, no longer acts as an AR agonist
; instead, it acts as an antagonist and disrupts AR signaling [11].
Thisinterpretationisbasedonexperimentsusingatwo-hybridprotein assay with Chinese hamster ovarian cells, in which natural or
pharmacologicARligandsaretestedfortheirabilitytoinduceinteraction between the amino and carboxyl termini (N/C) of the AR
[11,18]. Formation of an N/C complex distinguishes agonists from
antagonists. It is important to realize that the mechanistic significanceofdataobtainedwiththetwo-hybridproteinassayisunclear.
In this assay the amino and carboxyl termini are deleted from the
ARandfusedtotwootherproteins.Thus,physiologicintactARsare
notused.Furthermore,Kemppainenetal.[18]concludedthat“inhibitionoftheN/Cinteractiondoesnotnecessarilyreflecttheactivity
of an antagonist.” Therefore, drawing biological conclusions, much
lessclinicalconclusions,abouttheantagonisticeffectofMPAbased
on the N/C interaction is not warranted. Thus, the contention by
Burrell et al. [11] that at high doses MPA acts as an androgen and
inhibits breast cancer growth, while at low doses MPA acts as an
anti-androgen,presumablybycompetingwithDHT,andstimulates
breast cancer growth, is not supported rigorously by their data.
The belief that progesterone may play an etiologic role in the
development of breast cancer was based on the very early observations that during the luteal phase of the menstrual cycle there is
an increased proliferative activity in normal breast tissues, as compared to the proliferative phase [19]. A detailed in vitro study in
breast cancer models dealing with progestational and androgenic
properties of MPA indicated that in PR-positive cells MPA exerted
its pharmacologic effects through both PR-A and PR-B. In contrast,
some of the androgen signaling by DHT was mediated via PR-B
[20]. Although in these studies MPA was a more potent progestogen than progesterone, its activity (expression of several genes)
was qualitatively similar to that of progesterone. Interestingly, the
study further demonstrated that there was no difference between
low dose MPA and pharmacological (high dose) MPA. The authors
explained this by suggesting that the PRs were saturated even by
low dose MPA, similar to that used for HT; therefore, larger doses
would not have any additional effect. However, additional pharmacological effects of MPA may also be exerted by a non-genomic
mechanism via membrane receptors or by still to be identified
mechanisms.
Studies in those breast cancer models further showed that some
effects of MPA can also be exerted through the AR. There was
extensive gene regulatory overlap between DHT and MPA but not
with progesterone. These observations are in agreement with the
well known androgenic activity of MPA, and more importantly this
activityissimilartothatofDHT.Thesefindingsfurtherquestionthe
Tilley and Birrell hypothesis that MPA disrupts AR signaling in the
breast. Whether the increased breast cancer risk associated with
MPA is due to its progestational or androgenic properties remains
to be established.
3.2. Animal studies
Studies have reported similarities between the macaque and
human breast regarding genetic coding sequence identities, sex
steroid receptor expression, response to exogenous sex steroids,
and development of neoplastic mammary gland lesions [21]. These
characteristics support the predictive value of the macaque model
forevaluatingsomehormonaleffectsonthebreast.Inarandomized
crossover trial by Wood et al. [22] the effects of estradiol combined with either MPA or micronized progesterone were evaluated
on biomarkers of breast cancer risk in a postmenopausal primate
model. In the study, 26 ovariectomized adult female cynomolgus
( 8.0.0.2542.1302888742 PDF Extractor SDK EVALUATION)
F.Z. Stanczyk, B.R. Bhavnani / Journal of Steroid Biochemistry & Molecular Biology 142 (2014) 30–38 33
macaques were rotated randomly to daily oral treatment with (a)
placebo; (b) estradiol (1mg); (c) estradiol (1mg) combined with
progesterone (200mg); (d) estradiol (1mg) combined with MPA
(2.5mg). Each treatment lasted 2 months and was separated by a
one-monthwashoutperiod.Theprimaryoutcomemeasuredbreast
epithelial cell proliferation, as determined by expression of Ki67
nuclear antigen. Hormone effects on cellular proliferation in the
breast showed large variability in each of the proliferation markers
evaluated. Compared to placebo, estradiol plus MPA showed a significantly greater proliferation in lobular and ductal epithelium, as
wellasinintramammarygeneexpressionoftheproliferationmarkers Ki67 and cyclin B1, whereas estradiol and progesterone did not.
Althoughthesefindingssuggestthatmicronizedprogesteronehasa
more favorable effect on risk biomarkers of postmenopausal breast
cancer than MPA, and the macaque appears to be a useful model for
evaluating hormonal effects on the breast, results obtained in animal studies must be interpreted with caution before extrapolating
them to humans.
3.3. Breast epithelial cell proliferation
A number of studies have been published on the effect of
exogenousprogestogenscombinedwithestrogenonbreastepithelial cell proliferation in postmenopausal women. In a short-term
cross-sectional observational study by Hofseth et al. [23], it was
concluded that there was greater epithelial cell proliferation in the
terminal duct lobular unit of the breast in women who received HT
withestrogenplusMPAcomparedtoestrogenaloneornoHT.Major
limitations in this study include use of different types of estrogen
and different MPA doses in the hormone treatment groups, as well
as differences in postmenopausal status (natural vs. surgical). In a
short-term prospective, randomized clinical study by Murkes et al.
[24], the effects of two 28-day cycles of either oral CEE or percutaneous estradiol gel, with the addition of oral MPA or progesterone,
respectively, for the last 14 days of each 28 day cycle, were examined in breast cell proliferation or bel-2 protein levels. CEE/MPA
increased proliferation more than estradiol/progesterone, with
a borderline significant difference. Furthermore, both regimens
induced an increase in bcl-2, which is an apoptotic marker that
potentially reflects an anti-cancer effect. A major limitation in this
study is that both the estrogen and progestogen components were
differentandtheestrogenwasadministeredbydifferentroutesand
duration, making it difficult to attribute differences to one or the
other variable.
Presently, there is no compelling evidence demonstrating
greater breast cell proliferation by MPA compared to other progestogens [25]. Drawing comparative conclusions using data from
different studies, and different formulations, routes of administration
, and populations is inappropriate. Long-term studies
comparing different progestogens with a fixed type and dose of
estrogeninnaturallyhealthyandyoungerpostmenopausalwomen
are urgently needed.
3.4. Clinical studies
3.4.1. Mammographic density
Women with a greater mammographic density have a higher
riskofbreastcancer,andabout25%ofpostmenopausalwomentaking estrogen–progestogen therapy have an increase in their breast
density [26]. However, it is not certain that the short-term increase
in breast density with HT changes an individual’s risk of breast cancer. The increased breast density associated with postmenopausal
HT appears to be a transient, reversible change that is not consistent with a persistent effect on breast cell proliferation. After
HT is discontinued, breast density decreases rapidly. In the 3-year,
randomized, placebo-controlled PEPI trial, almost all increases in
breast density occurred in the first year, regardless of whether the
regimen was continuous (0.625mg CEE/2.5mg MPA) or sequential (0.625mg CEE combined with either 10mg MPA or 200mg
micronized progesterone for the last 12 days per month) [27]. The
usersofestrogen–progestogencombinedregimenshada5to7-fold
greater increase in breast density compared with users of estrogen
(CEE)alone.Nosignificantdifferencewasobservedwhenthebreast
densityincreaseswerecomparedbetweentheMPAandmicronized
progesterone-containing regimens.
3.4.2. Observational studies
The contribution of MPA to absolute breast cancer risk has
been difficult to assess in epidemiologic studies, which often
involve multiple types of progestogens and routes of concomitant estrogen therapy. A substantial number of case–control and
cohort studies published over a 15-year period from 1988 to 2003
reported mixed results regarding increased risk of breast cancer with estrogen–progestin treatment in postmenopausal women
[26]. However, overall, most of the studies demonstrated a significantly greater risk associated with estrogen–progestin treatment
compared to treatment with estrogen alone, with a relative risk
that in most instances was well under 2.0. Most of the studies
were performed in the United States and therefore primarily analyzed CEE with or without MPA, which is the most commonly used
HT in this country. In Europe, however, the most commonly used
HT preparations are based on estradiol and a variety of different
progestogens.
In a review of data from 8 randomized controlled trials and
19 epidemiologic studies, which were designed to determine
whether there is an association between postmenopausal hormonal treatment and risk of breast cancer, data from both study
designs reported similar conclusions regarding use of estrogen or
estrogen–progestogenandriskofbreastcancer[28].Theriskfactor
wasfoundtobehigherwithuseofestrogen–progestogenthanwith
use of estrogen alone. In addition, the results showed that breast
cancerriskdoesnotvaryaccordingtothetypeofhormonalproduct,
dosage,orrouteofadministration,butcontinuousprogestogenregimens may involve more risk than sequential regimens (estrogen
followed by progestogen for 10–12 days of each 28-day cycle).
Two notable epidemiologic studies carried out in Europe in
recent years that have addressed breast cancer risk associated with
the most commonly used estrogen–progestogen formulations are
theMillionWomen StudyandE3NCohortStudy.Theobservational
Million Women Study [29] recruited one million postmenopausal
women between 1996 and 2001 from those invited to have screening mammograms every 3 years in the United Kingdom National
Health Service Breast Screening Program. Data for this study were
obtainedfromquestionnairesreturnedbeforetheinitialmammography
, and the women were followed to determine the incidence
of cancer and death. The study found no significant differences
in breast cancer risk between estrogen alone (RR=1.30; 95% confidence intervals (CI): 1.22–1.38) and estrogen combined with
various progestins, including MPA (RR=1.60; 95% CI: 1.33–1.93),
norethindrone (RR=1.53; 95% CI: 1.35–1.75) and levonorgestrel
(RR=1.97; 95% CI: 1.74–2.33).
The E3N cohort study examined the association between different HT regimens and breast cancer risk in 80,377 younger
postmenopausal women (mean age 53.1 years) who lived in France
andwereinsuredbyahealthinsuranceplancoveringmostlyteachers [30]. The women independently completed questionnaires to
assess medical history, menopausal status, and lifestyle characteristics at 1–3 year intervals during an average 8.1 year follow-up
between 1990 and 2002. Nine progestogens including MPA were
used in combination with oral or transdermal estrogen for HT. The
oral estrogen was predominantly micronized estradiol; CEE was
only rarely used. All estrogen/progestogen combinations except
(8.0.0.2542,1295797809 PDF Extractor SDK TRIAL)
34 F.Z. Stanczyk, B.R. Bhavnani / Journal of Steroid Biochemistry & Molecular Biology 142 (2014) 30–38
for estrogen–progesterone and estrogen–dydrogesterone showed
substantial increases in breast cancer risk. No significant differences were observed among the different progestogens that were
associated with the increased risk, regardless of whether they were
androgenic, non-androgenic or anti-androgenic. Furthermore, the
relativeriskinthewomentakingMPAplusoralestrogen(1.48;95%
CI: 1.02–1.06) was similar to that in the oral estrogen alone users
(1.32; 95% CI: 0.76–2.29). Therefore, based on the data obtained in
theE3Ncohortstudy,itappearsthatMPAdoesnotappreciablycontribute to the effects of estrogen alone that would be biologically
or clinically relevant.
A relatively smaller case–control study in which different HT
preparations were compared to one another was performed within
Germany[31].Inthisstudyatotalof3,593breastcancercaseswere
identified and compared with 9,098 controls. Although the sample
sizewastoosmallforvalidsub-analysisofsomeofthegroupsusing
different HT formulations, the breast cancer risk generally did not
vary much among the groups. Also, no evidence was found to confirm one of the hypotheses of the study that CEE combined with
MPA may be associated with a different risk compared to formulations using other estrogens, e.g., micronized estradiol or estradiol
valerate, with or without MPA. The relative risk for MPA combined
with estrogen was 0.96 (95% CI=0.7–1.3), based on 138 cases and
340 controls.
3.4.3. Randomized Controlled Trials
Considerable concern about the safety of MPA arose from the
findings of the placebo-controlled WHI trial. This trial consisted
of 16,608 postmenopausal women, ages 50–79 years, who were
randomized according to hysterectomy status. Women with an
intact uterus were randomized to oral daily continuous combined 0.625mg CEE plus 2.5mg MPA (Prempro®) (N=8506) or
placebo (N=8102), whereas women who had a prior hysterectomy
were randomized to oral daily 0.625mg CEE alone (Premarin®)
(N=5310) or placebo (N=3429) [2,3]. Although the initial planned
duration of the trial was 8.5 years, the CEE/MPA arm of the trial
was discontinued prematurely after 5.2 years primarily because
of increased risk of breast cancer (hazard ratio of 1.26 (95% CI:
1.0–1.6)) when the treatment group was compared to the placebo
group[2];therewasnoincreasedbreastcancerriskintheestrogenonly arm [3]. In an updated WHI report on invasive breast cancer
risk,theestrogen–progestinarmshowedlittlechangeinthehazard
ratio (1.24; CI: 1.01–1.54) from the one published earlier [32].
It is important to realize that the findings of the WHI trial generally agree with the results of the case–control and cohort studies
discussed earlier, indicating that current use of combined estrogen
and progestin has a small increased risk of breast cancer compared
with use of estrogen alone. This is consistent with the position of
theU.S.FoodandDrugAdministration.Thewarninglanguageinthe
package insert of Prempro® states that in the absence of comparable data, the increased risk of invasive breast cancer demonstrated
in the estrogen–progestin arm of the WHI trial should be assumed
tobesimilarnotonlyforotherdosesofCEEandMPA,butalsoother
combinations and dosage forms of estrogens and progestins.
4. Effect of MPA on cardiovascular disease risk
4.1. Coronary heart disease in clinical studies
The cardioprotective effects of estrogens had been a primary
justification for postmenopausal HT, however, long-term clinical
studies of MPA combined with CEE, including the WHI trial [2]
and Heart and Estrogen–Progestin Replacement Study (HERS) [33]
have largely concluded that there is no overall benefit in terms
of a reduction in coronary heart disease (CHD) events. Secondary
analyses of the WHI data [34,35] have stratified CHD risk by years
since menopause, and found a reduced CHD risk in the women who
started HT within 10 years of menopause compared with women
initiating HT more than 10 years after the onset of menopause.
However, among women within 10 years of menopause, the risk
of CHD with HT compared to placebo was greatest within the first
2 years of treatment (hazard ratio 1.29 for the first 2 years vs. 0.64
for the first 8 years) and a cardioprotective effect of HT in these
women was not apparent until after 6 years of treatment [34]. As
a result of this lack of cardiovascular benefit, HT is only recommended for short-term treatment of menopausal symptoms in the
early years following the onset of menopause.
4.2. Effects of risk markers
Whether or not HT itself contributes to the development of CHD
is not clear. A nested case–control study of the 359 incident CHD
events during the first 4 years of follow-up in the WHI trial [36]
assessed a range of inflammatory, lipid, thrombotic, and genetic
markers of CHD risk. Although HT was associated with changes in
some biomarker levels, none could be linked with the incidence
of future CHD events. Women with higher LDL-cholesterol levels
prior to HT treatment had a significantly greater risk of CHD with
CEE plus MPA treatment compared to no treatment, and a nonsignificant protective effect against CHD was seen for women with
higher HDL-cholesterol levels prior to treatment. However, these
are known risk factors for CHD that could have contributed to the
presence of sub-clinical coronary artery disease prior to initiating
HT. A case–control study also examined the presence of metabolic
syndromeatbaseline[37].AsignificantlygreaterriskofCHDevents
was seen with CEE/MPA compared to placebo in participants with
pre-existing metabolic syndrome (odds ratio (OR)=2.26; 95% CI:
1.26–4.07), while women without metabolic syndrome prior to
startingtreatmenthadnoincreasedriskofCHDwithHT(OR=0.97;
95% CI: 0.58–1.61).
Both oral and parenteral estrogen treatments are known to
raise HDL-cholesterol and to lower total and LDL-cholesterol. MPA
opposes this beneficial effect, notably the rise in HDL-cholesterol,
which has been attributed to its androgenic properties [38,39].
However, no net reduction in HDL-cholesterol compared to levels without estrogen treatment has been seen, and there is no clear
link between altered lipid profiles as a result of MPA treatment
and clinical cardiovascular disease. Some of the protective effects
ofCEE/MPAmaybeexertedbyinhibitionofLDLoxidationandthese
aspects are discussed elsewhere in this special issue.
It is unusual to find studies of MPA alone compared to CEE. One
study of 33 women undergoing surgical menopause (hysterectomy
with bilateral oophorectomy) examined the effect of 10mg/day
MPA (18 women) or 0.6mg/day CEE (15 women) for 12 months
after surgery on lipid profiles, blood sugar control, hormones,
and inflammatory markers [40]. Increases in BMI, triglycerides,
HDL-cholesterol, and C-reactive protein after 1 year compared to
baseline values were greater in the CEE-treated women than in the
MPA-treatedwomen,andincreasesinserumalbuminweregreater
intheMPAgroup.WhiletheincreaseinHDL-cholesterolwithestrogen treatment has often been reported, and could be protective
against CHD, MPA would appear to have a more beneficial effect
on albumin, triglycerides, and CRP, suggesting a less inflammatory
state.However,limitationsofthisstudyincludethemodestsample
size, and lack of a control group and baseline measures.
4.3. Effects of vascular and cardiac function
In terms of vascular function, estrogen therapy in postmenopausal women has been observed to improve endotheliumdependent vasodilation, and is one of the mechanisms by which
(8.0.0.2542.1288706876 PDF Extractor SDK TRIAL VERSIOn)
F.Z. Stanczyk, B.R. Bhavnani / Journal of Steroid Biochemistry & Molecular Biology 142 (2014) 30–38 35
estrogen is believed to reduce cardiovascular disease risk. Some
progestogens have been found to mitigate this improvement [41].
Studiesoftheeffectsofsteroidsonvascularfunctionhavebeenconducted in non-human primates, whose blood vessel characteristics
are closer to those of humans than non-primate animals. A study
in cynomolgus monkeys [42] found coronary artery dilation with
CEE treatment, which was reduced by 50% when either cyclic or
continuous MPA was administered concomitantly. In rhesus monkeys
, administration of MPA alone was found to have an adverse
effect on coronary artery hyperreactivity [43]. The beneficial effect
of estrogen treatment in protecting against coronary vasospasm,
induced via increased protein kinase C activation resulting in sustainedintracellularcalciumelevation,wasalsofoundtobenegated
by MPA [44,45]. However, a review by Clarkson and Appt [46]
examinedstudiesofvascularreactivityinpostmenopausalwomen,
which used brachial artery flow-mediated dilation, and concluded
that the majority of these showed no reduction in benefit when
MPA was administered in addition to CEE. Clarkson and Appt also
reviewed the effects of HT in women on markers of inflammation,
includingproinflammatorycytokines,celladhesionmolecules,and
C-reactiveprotein,andconcludedthattheadditionofMPAdoesnot
appear to attenuate any beneficial effect of estrogen treatment.
Recent studies of aspects of cardiovascular function have also
found no deleterious effect of MPA when combined with estrogen
treatment. To determine whether MPA has any negative impact on
improvedbloodflowinestrogen-treatedwomen,Wenderetal.[47]
measured the pulsatility index (PI) in the internal carotid artery of
75 postmenopausal women before and after 16 weeks of treatment
with CEE (0.625mg/day) either alone or with MPA (2.5mg/day).
The PI was significantly reduced in both groups, with no difference
between the 2 treatment groups.
Duzenli et al. [48] used tissue Doppler echocardiography to
determine the myocardial performance index (MPI) in 60 postmenopausalwomenaged45–55,tostudythedirectimpactofHTon
cardiac function. The HT group comprised 32 women who received
0.625mg CEE plus 2.5mg MPA per day for 6 months, while 28 control women received no HT. Myocardial velocity, MPI and exercise
durationwereevaluatedatbaselineand3and6monthsafterstarting treatment. There was an improvement in diastolic function in
the HT group and a significant increase in exercise capacity after 3
months of treatment, which persisted through the end of the study
period. No changes were seen in the control women.
4.4. Effects on clotting
It is well recognized that oral postmenopausal HT, including
estrogen alone or estrogen plus progestogen, increases the risk of
venous thromboembolism (VTE). However, whether addition of a
progestogen to estrogen for HT use increases VTE risk is still disputed. A systematic review and meta-analysis of 8 observational
studies and 9 randomized controlled trials showed no significant
difference in the risk of VTE between unopposed oral estrogen
(pooled OR=2.2; 95% CI, 1.6–3.0) and oral estrogen plus progestogen (OR=2.6; 95% CI 2.0–3.2) [49]. In a multicenter case–control
study of VTE (Estrogen and Thromboembolism Risk (ESTHER)
study) among postmenopausal women, in which 271 cases were
matched with 610 controls, the ORs for VTE in current users of
oral and transdermal estrogen were 4.2 (95% CI: 1.5–11.6) and 0.9
(95% CI: 0.4–2.1), respectively [50]. There was no significant association of VTE with either micronized progesterone or pregnane
derivatives, including MPA (OR=0.7; 95% CI: 0.3–0.9 and OR, 0.9;
95% CI: 0.4–2.3, respectively). In contrast, norpregnane derivatives
(promegestone,nomegestrolacetate)wereassociatedwitha4-fold
increased VTE risk (OR=3.9; 95% CI: 1.5–10.0).
Other studies show that VTE is more prevalent when the combinedHTincludesMPAcomparedtootherprogestogens.Sweetland
et al. [51] evaluated the incidence of VTE in the Million Women
Study, in which 2200 women out of the 1,058,259 enrolled in the
study had a diagnosis of VTE. Comparing HT users with never users
inthestudy,therelativeriskofVTEwas2.07(95%CI:1.86–2.31)for
oral estrogen/progestin treatment and 1.42 (95% CI: 1.21–1.66) for
oral estrogen only, while transdermal estrogen-only treatment did
not increase risk of VTE (RR=0.82; 95% CI: 0.64–1.06). When the
type of progestin used in combined HT was investigated, the RR for
MPA was significantly greater than for other progestins (RR=2.67;
95% CI: 2.25–3.17 vs. 1.91; 95% CI: 1.69–2.17). No significant association was seen between duration of HT use and risk of VTE. In
the Women’s International Study of long Duration Estrogen after
Menopause (WISDOM) trial, the results showed that users of CEE
plus MPA had a higher thrombotic risk than those who used CEE
alone; however, the difference in VTE risk did not reach statistical significance (HR=2.39; 95%/CI: 0.62–9.24) [52]. In a study by
Canonico et al. [53], there was uncertainty regarding the impact of
pregnane and 19-nortestosterone derivatives on VTE risk (OR=1.3;
95% CI: 0.9–2.0 and OR=1.4; 95% CI=0.7–2.4, respectively), partly
due to lack of statistical power for subgroup analysis.
ItisimportanttorealizethattheactualVTEriskinwomenisvery
low due to the low incidence of this event. Among healthy low-risk
women, even after the age of 40, the VTE risk is probably only 1–2
cases per 10,000 women per year [54]. If there is a 2-fold increase
in relative risk of VTE, this would increase the VTE incidence by
approximately 2 cases per 10,000 women per year of HT use.
5. Effect of MPA on the brain
Progesteroneisanimportantneurosteroid,andithasneuroprotective effects when used to treat traumatic brain injury [55,56]. In
brain-injured rats, MPA administration showed some benefit by
reducing cerebral edema in a dose-dependent fashion, but unlike
progesterone did not enhance behavioral recovery, indicated by a
lack of improvement in spatial learning performance [57].
Studies in rat hippocampal cells indicate that MPA not only
does not share the neuroprotective effects of progesterone, it
also antagonizes the neuroprotective effects of estradiol [58–60].
Ciriza et al. [61] studied the effects of progesterone metabolism
on neuroprotection in rats and found that both of the two metabolites 5-dihydroprogesterone and allopregnanolone were required
for its neuroprotective activity against the effects of kainic acid,
which induces excitotoxic neuronal death. In their model, progesterone
, but not MPA, increased levels of these progesterone
metabolitesandpreventedthekainicacid-inducedcelldeath.However
, when studying the effects on brain tissue of progesterone and
MPA administered orally to ovariectomized rats in vivo, Bernardi
et al. [62] found that both progestogens increased tissue allopregnanolone levels in the frontal and parietal lobes, hypothalamus,
hippocampus,andanteriorpituitary;wheneitherprogestogenwas
combined with estradiol valerate, allopregnanolone was increased
to a greater extent than with estradiol valerate alone.
Jodhka et al. [63] have found that progesterone, but not MPA,
protected against glutamate toxicity in explants of the cerebral
cortex of mice, and that this protective effect was mediated by
induction of brain-derived neurotrophic factor (BDNF); progesterone increased BDNF mRNA expression and BDNF levels in the
explants, whereas MPA reduced both. The effects of MPA on glycolysis
, oxidative stress, and mitochondrial function were studied
in brains of ovariectomized rats by Irwin et al. [64]. They found
that MPA antagonized the post-ovariectomy restoration of mitochondrial function by estradiol, whereas in a previous study they
had found that both progesterone and estradiol improved brain
mitochondrial function in ovariectomized rats [65]. Whether similiar changes will be observed in healthy postmenopausal women

36 F.Z. Stanczyk, B.R. Bhavnani / Journal of Steroid Biochemistry & Molecular Biology 142 (2014) 30–38
who are on HT remains to be investigated. Therefore, the clinical
significance of these animal studies remains to be established.
Long-term clinical effects of oral MPA on neurological function in older postmenopausal women have been observed in
the Women’s Health Initiative Memory Study (WHIMS), which
recruited 4532 women older than 65 years of age who were
enrolled in the WHI study of postmenopausal HT and were free
of dementia [66]. After a mean follow-up of 4.05 years, 61 of the
women were diagnosed with probable dementia; 40 were in the
CEE/MPA group and 21 in the placebo group (HR=2.05, 95% CI:
1.21–3.48). A substantial decline in cognitive function, assessed
using the modified mini-mental state examination (MMSE), was
observed in 6.7% of the CEE/MPA group compared to 4.8% of the
placebo group [67]; the relative odds of a decline in the MMSE
score increased as the magnitude of the decline in score increased;
thus there was a greater frequency of large declines in MMSE score
in the CEE/MPA group, representing a small subset of women who
experiencedacleardetrimentaleffect.Ifjustthosewomenwiththe
largestdecreaseinscorewereremovedfromtheanalysis,theoverall group differences were not significant. Also, in a subset of 1403
womenintheWHIMSstudyforwhobrainMRIscanswereobtained,
hippocampal volume, indicating brain atrophy, was reduced in the
women treated with CEE either with or without MPA compared to
placebo [68].
A systematic review [69] examined declines in verbal memory,
a predictor of Alzheimer’s disease, in randomized clinical trials of
postmenopausal HT. In both younger and older postmenopausal
women, CEE/MPA appears to have a detrimental effect on verbal
memory, while estrogen-only therapy was beneficial in younger
women (<65 years old).
Both animal and human studies therefore do not support a beneficial role for MPA in terms of brain function, and it appears to be
associated with detrimental effects on the brain. However, studies
are needed on the effects of MPA plus estrogen on brain function
in women within 5 years of entering menopause, since this is the
period when HT is most strongly recommended for treatment of
menopausal symptoms. Most of the clinical observations on the
effects of HT on brain function have been made in older postmenopausal women, in whom declines in cognitive function and
memory can be more readily observed. These aspects are discussed
in greater detail by others in this issue.
6. Conclusions
The negative results of large clinical studies employing combined HT with MPA and CEE in postmenopausal women has called
into question the safety of MPA. This review examines the pharmacokinetics and metabolism of MPA, which can be altered by a
variety of factors in the age group of women using HT to produce
potential changes in potency. It also looks at the effects of MPA on
the breast, cardiovascular system, and brain, to examine whether
there is evidence for an increase in risk of diseases in these areas
with MPA use.
Regarding effects of MPA on the breast, the contention that at
high doses MPA acts as an androgen and inhibits breast cancer
growth, whereas at low doses it acts as an anti-androgen and
stimulates breast cancer growth, is not supported by the published
data. Studies in the macaque have found increased breast cell
proliferation with MPA compared to micronized progesterone, but
clinical studies are lacking that compare the effects of different
progestogenswhilekeepingthetypeanddoseofestrogenconstant.
Increases in mammographic density have been reported with both
MPA and micronized progesterone, but this is reversible and may
not translate to increased breast cell proliferation leading to breast
cancer. While studies are lacking that directly attribute breast
cancer risk to MPA, randomized controlled trials and observational
studies show a higher risk with use of estrogen-progestogen than
with estrogen alone. In addition, the results generally show that
breast cancer risk does not vary according to the type of hormonal
product, dosage, or route of administration.
A number of factors contribute to increased risk of cardiovasculardisease,includingeffectsonbloodlipids,inflammatorymarkers,
clotting, and vascular function. Some primate studies have found
thatMPAnegatesthebeneficialeffectsofestradiolonvascularfunction
, but these results have not been confirmed in human studies.
MPA has typically androgenic effects on blood lipids, but whether
or not these eventually translate into the development of cardiovascular disease has not been established. Randomized controlled
trials and observational studies generally show no significant difference in the risk of VTE between unopposed oral estrogen and
oral estrogen plus progestogen.
In vitro studies suggest that MPA antagonizes the neuroprotective effects of estradiol. Also, clinical studies in older women
have found a decline in cognitive function with use of MPA in combined HT compared to placebo. However, negative effects of MPA
in the age group most commonly prescribed postmenopausal HT
for symptom relief have not been demonstrated.
In light of the evidence presented in this review, overall, it
appears that short-term (up to 5 years) use of MPA in combined HT in the years immediately following menopause for the
management of vasomotor symptoms in otherwise healthy postmenopausal can be considered safe. Whether progesterone and
other progestogens will have a more favorable pharmacological
profileremainstobeestablished.Long-termstudiescomparingdifferenttypes,dosesandroutesofadministrationofpostmenopausal
HT, including MPA, are warranted.

her assistance with preparation of the manuscript.
